reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Performs a 3D transposed convolution operation with square input and square kernel,
    and supports padding, dilation, and stride.

    Args:
        in_channels (int): Number of channels in the input tensor.
        out_channels (int): Number of channels produced by the convolution.
        kernel_size (int): Size of the convolution kernel (square kernel, so only one value needed).
        stride (int, optional): Stride of the convolution. Defaults to 1.
        padding (int, optional): Padding applied to the input. Defaults to 0.
        dilation (int, optional): Spacing between kernel elements. Defaults to 1.
        bias (bool, optional): If `True`, adds a learnable bias to the output. Defaults to `False`.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels: int, out_channels: int, kernel_size: int, stride: int = 1, padding: int = 0, dilation: int = 1, bias: bool = False):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose3d = nn.ConvTranspose3d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=(kernel_size, kernel_size, kernel_size), stride=stride, padding=padding, dilation=dilation, bias=bias)

    def forward(self, x: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Performs the 3D transposed convolution.

        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_channels, depth_out, height_out, width_out).
        """
        return self.conv_transpose3d(x)

# Test code
batch_size = 16
in_channels = 32
out_channels = 64
kernel_size = 3
depth = 16
height = 32
width = 32
stride = 2
padding = 1
dilation = 2

def get_inputs():
    x = torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, depth, height, width)
    return [x]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, dilation]

Let's check the transposed convolution output size formula from PyTorch docs: `L_out = (L_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1` This is indeed the formula. For L_in=16, stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2, kernel_size=3, output_padding=0: L_out = 15*2 -2 + 4 + 1 = 33. Good. Now, what is the actual mapping? I think the confusion arises because we are thinking of transposed conv as "scattering" input to output, but the scattering might be offset differently. Let's look at the standard algorithm for transposed convolution: Given input size W, filter size K, stride S, padding P. The transposed conv can be computed as: 1. Compute the size of the output if padding were zero: W' = (W - 1) * S + K. 2. The output with padding is of size W_out = W' - 2P. 3. The output index j corresponds to input index i and filter index k such that j = i*S + k - P. This is exactly what we had. But in our case, dilation adds spacing between filter taps. The effective filter size K_eff = 1 + (K-1)*dilation. So W' = (W-1)*S + K_eff. Then W_out = W' - 2P. And j = i*S + k*dilation - P. Plugging numbers: W=16, S=2, K=3, dilation=2 => K_eff = 1 + 2*2 = 5. Output size formula for transposed convolution: O = (I - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 Often TransposedConv3d uses `output_padding=0` by default. Given input shape (N, C_in, D, H, W). Let kernel be (K, K, K). We can think of transposed convolution as a normal convolution of an upsampled input with interleaved zeros. Specifically, with stride s, padding p, dilation d. ConvTranspose3d with stride s, padding p, dilation d is equivalent to: 1. Create an upsampled input of shape (N, C_in, D', H', W') where D' = (D-1)*s + 1, same for H', W'. Place original input values at positions 0, s, 2s, ... and zeros elsewhere. 2. Then apply a Conv3d with same kernel_size, dilation=d, padding = d*(kernel_size-1) - p? Let's derive. Actually ConvTranspose3d with parameters (stride, padding, dilation, output_padding) is the transpose of Conv3d with same parameters? In PyTorch, ConvTranspose is gradient of Conv. For Conv3d with stride s, padding p, dilation d, kernel size k, the transpose operation (ConvTranspose3d) output size is: O = (I - 1) * s - 2*p + d*(k - 1) + output_padding + 1 The effective kernel size for transposed conv is k' = k. The transposed conv can be computed as normal conv on the dilated input with certain padding. But a simpler approach: implement ConvTranspose3d directly as the sum over input positions and kernel weights. For each output position (n, c_out, z, y, x), value = sum_{c_in, kz, ky, kx} weight[c_out, c_in, kz, ky, kx] * (corresponding input value) + bias[c_out] (if bias). We need to map output coordinates back to input coordinates using transposed convolution indexing. In PyTorch transposed convolution, the output value at position (n, c_out, o_d, o_h, o_w) is computed by considering which input positions contribute. For a standard Conv3d with stride S, padding P, dilation D, the output coordinate o maps from input coordinate i and kernel offset k via: o = (i * S) - P + k*D ??? Wait, for standard conv (not transposed), the formula is: out = (in + 2*P - D*(K-1) - 1) / S + 1 And for each output spatial index o, the input spatial index i is in range: i = o*S - P + k*D for k in [0, K-1]. Specifically, for a standard convolution: for each output position o: acc = 0 for k in 0..K-1: i = o * stride - padding + k * dilation if i in [0, in_size-1]: acc += input[i] * weight[k] For transposed convolution (the gradient w.r.t input of standard conv), the output is larger. The mapping is reversed: for each input position i, it contributes to output positions o such that i = o*stride - padding + k*dilation for some kernel offset k. Solving for o: o = (i + padding - k*dilation) / stride. But this must be integer. However, in transposed convolution, the forward operation (which is the transpose of conv) can be seen as: we have an input x of size I, and we want to produce output y of size O such that the operation is adjoint of conv. A more direct formulation for transposed conv forward: It is equivalent to convolving an upsampled (with zeros) and padded input with the kernel, but with flipped kernel? Actually, looking at PyTorch docs: - Conv3d weight: (out_channels, in_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW) - ConvTranspose3d weight: (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW) And the operation can be expressed as: for each output location (z, y, x): for each input location (z', y', x') and kernel offset (kz, ky, kx): if (z', y', x') * stride + something = ... Hmm. Another way: Transposed convolution is equivalent to the gradient of a convolution. The gradient w.r.t input of conv (with given stride, padding, dilation) is a conv_transpose operation. The gradient w.r.t input of conv is computed by convolving the output gradient (which has the shape of conv output) with the flipped/rotated kernel using full convolution / transposed parameters. Perhaps easiest: implement manual transposed convolution formula by mapping output coordinates to input coordinates using the same formula as the gradient of conv. Let's derive from first principles. Standard 1D convolution (for simplicity) with input x[0..I-1], weight w[0..K-1], stride S, pad P, dilation D. Output size O = floor((I + 2P - D*(K-1) - 1) / S) + 1 For each output index o (0..O-1): out[o] = sum_{k=0}^{K-1} x[ i ] * w[k] where i = o * S - P + k * D only if 0 <= i < I. Now transposed convolution (call it T) takes an input u[0..O-1] and produces output v[0..I-1] (or some larger size defined by transpose formula? Wait, the transpose of a linear map from R^I to R^O is a map from R^O to R^I. If conv maps input of size I to output of size O, the transpose maps input of size O to output of size I. But ConvTranspose in PyTorch maps from "input size" I' to "output size" O' using parameters such that O' = (I' -1)*S - 2P + D*(K-1) + 1 (with default output_padding=0). That O' is exactly the size of the original input to conv if the conv output size was I'. Wait, let's verify. If we have a conv with input size = O', output size = I'. Then: I' = floor((O' + 2P - D*(K-1) - 1)/S) + 1. Then the transpose of that conv maps from size I' back to O'. The formula for ConvTranspose output size given input I' is: O' = (I' - 1) * S - 2P + D*(K-1) + 1. Let's check if these are consistent: I' = floor(( (I'-1)*S - 2P + D*(K-1) + 1 + 2P - D*(K-1) - 1 ) / S ) + 1 = floor(( (I'-1)*S ) / S) + 1 = (I'-1) + 1 = I'. Yes, it's consistent: conv then conv_transpose with same parameters gives identity on sizes (but not on values because of weight shape difference). So transpose conv from size I' to O' can be thought of as the adjoint of conv from O' to I'. The adjoint operation (backprop w.r.t input) is: Given u of size I' (this is the "input" to transpose conv), and weight w (shape (in_ch, out_ch, ...)), compute v of size O' (output of transpose conv). The formula for backprop w.r.t input of conv: if forward conv is y = conv(x; w) with x size O', y size I', then gradient w.r.t x is: grad_x[o'] = sum_{o, k} grad_y[o] * w[k] where o' = o*S - P + k*D, for all o,k such that 0 <= o' < O'. This looks exactly like scattering: for each output position o of the conv (which is input to transpose conv), we spread its value to multiple input positions o' using the kernel. Therefore, the forward of ConvTranspose can be computed as: For each input position i (in the transpose conv input): For each kernel offset k: output_position = i * stride - padding + k * dilation if output_position in valid range of output: output[output_position] += input[i] * weight[k] Wait, but we need to be careful: in the conv forward, x[o'] contributes to y[o] with weight w[k] where o' = o*S - P + k*D. So y[o] = sum_{k, o' = o*S - P + k*D} x[o'] * w[k]. The transpose operation maps y to x: x[o'] = sum_{o, k} y[o] * w[k] where o' = o*S - P + k*D, so for a given o', the sum is over all (o,k) satisfying that equation. Equivalently, for each y[o], we add y[o] * w[k] to x[o*S - P + k*D]. So indeed: for each input index o (this is the transpose conv input): for each kernel offset k: out_pos = o * stride - padding + k * dilation if 0 <= out_pos < output_size: output[out_pos] += input[o] * weight[k] This holds for 1D. In 3D, for each input position (d, h, w), and each kernel offset (kd, kh, kw): od = d * stride - padding + kd * dilation oh = h * stride - padding + kh * dilation ow = w * stride - padding + kw * dilation if all in bounds: output[n, c_out, od, oh, ow] += input[n, c_in, d, h, w] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] Note: PyTorch ConvTranspose3d weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). So the indexing is weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]. Wait, is there a flip? In the standard Conv3d, weight shape is (out_channels, in_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). The transpose conv weight shape is (in_channels, out_channels/groups, kD, kH, kW). The backprop formula uses the same weight values, not flipped. So the above formula should be correct. Let's verify with a simple example manually. Consider 1D, input size 2, stride=2, padding=0, kernel_size=2, dilation=1. Input u = [u0, u1]. Weight w = [w0, w1] (shape (1,1,2) for convtranspose). Using the scatter formula: For input pos 0: out_pos = 0*2 + k*1 = {0,1} For input pos 1: out_pos = 1*2 + k*1 = {2,3} So output = [u0*w0, u0*w1, u1*w0, u1*w1]. Now let's see what PyTorch does: ```python m = nn.ConvTranspose1d(1,1,2,stride=2,bias=False) m.weight.data[:] = w u = torch.tensor([[[u0,u1]]]) out = m(u) ``` PyTorch's ConvTranspose1d output shape for input (N, C, L) = (1,1,2) is (1,1,4). Let's compute manually what PyTorch does. I recall that ConvTranspose1d effectively computes cross-correlation (like conv1d) but transposed. In standard conv1d, output at pos o = sum_k input[o*S - P + k*D] * w[k]. Transpose means output at pos o' gets contributions from input[pos o] where o' = o*S - P + k*D. So for stride=2, P=0, D=1: o' = o*2 + k. Yes, so out[0] = u0*w0, out[1]=u0*w1, out[2]=u1*w0, out[3]=u1*w1. This matches. Now, what about padding? If padding=1 for transposed conv. Output size = (2-1)*2 - 2*1 + 1 + 1 = 2. So length 2. Scatter formula: out_pos = o*2 - 1 + k. For o=0: out_pos = -1,0 => only out_pos=0 valid (k=1) => out[0] += u0*w1 For o=1: out_pos = 1,2 => only out_pos=1 valid (k=0) => out[1] += u1*w0 So output = [u0*w1, u1*w0]. Let's verify with PyTorch? I'm fairly confident. Now, about the equivalence with standard conv on upsampled input: Upsampled input (stride=2): length = (2-1)*2+1 = 3: [u0, 0, u1]. Then conv1d with kernel_size=2, stride=1, padding = D*(K-1) - P = 1 - 1 = 0. Conv output: out[0] = u0*w0 + 0*w1 = u0*w0. out[1] = 0*w0 + u1*w1 = u1*w1. Wait, that gives [u0*w0, u1*w1]. That's different! Something is off. Ah! The weight used in standard conv is (out, in, k), i.e., w' of shape (1,1,2). But in transposed conv, weight shape is (in, out, k). If we just permute first two dims, w' = w.transpose(0,1). Since in=out=1, it's same. But the result [u0*w0, u1*w1] is not matching transposed conv result [u0*w1, u1*w0]. Why? Because transposed conv corresponds to convolving the upsampled input with the *flipped* kernel (since standard conv is cross-correlation). In PyTorch, conv1d is actually cross-correlation: output[t] = sum_k input[t + k] * w[k]? Let's verify PyTorch conv formula. PyTorch Conv1d documentation: out[N, C_out, L] = sum_k weight[C_out, C_in, k] * input[N, C_in, stride*L + k - padding]. Wait, the exact formula for 1D convolution in PyTorch is: out[i] = sum_k input[i * stride - padding + k * dilation] * weight[k] This is cross-correlation, not convolution (no flipping). So the backprop w.r.t input uses the same orientation of weight (no flipping) because cross-correlation's adjoint uses the same weight. Let's check the manual calculation of standard conv on upsampled input. Upsampled input x' = [u0, 0, u1] at positions 0,1,2. Conv with stride=1, padding=0, kernel size 2, no dilation. out_conv[0] = x'[0]*w[0] + x'[1]*w[1] = u0*w0. out_conv[1] = x'[1]*w[0] + x'[2]*w[1] = u1*w1. But transposed conv gave [u0*w1, u1*w0]. So taking the same w doesn't match. However, if we reverse w (i.e., use w[1], w[0]) then: out_conv[0] = u0*w1 + 0*w0 = u0*w1. out_conv[1] = 0*w1 + u1*w0 = u1*w0. That matches. But does adjoint of cross-correlation involve flipping the kernel? Let's derive more carefully. For a linear operator A represented by cross-correlation with kernel w (no flip): (Ax)[o] = sum_k x[o*S - P + k] * w[k]. The adjoint A^T satisfies <y, Ax> = <A^T y, x>. Left side: sum_o y[o] * sum_k x[o*S - P + k] * w[k]. Swap sums: sum_{o,k} y[o] * w[k] * x[o*S - P + k]. Let i = o*S - P + k. For a fixed i, o can be any integer such that o = (i + P - k)/S. The sum over (o,k) with i = o*S - P + k is equivalent to sum_{k} sum_{o: i = o*S - P + k} y[o] * w[k] * x[i]. Thus (A^T y)[i] = sum_k y[o(i,k)] * w[k] where o(i,k) = (i + P - k)/S (if integer and valid). This is exactly the scatter formula we used earlier, no flip of w. So why did the upsample+conv approach suggest a flip? Because that approach said "apply a standard conv with padding = D*(K-1) - P" but didn't mention that the weight might need to be flipped or that the conv used should be true convolution, not cross-correlation. Let's check the standard equivalence again. In many deep learning frameworks, transposed convolution is implemented as conv on dilated input with flipped kernel. But since PyTorch conv is cross-correlation, maybe we need to use conv with flipped kernel (which is equivalent to convolution). Let's test with the earlier example. If we flip w -> w_flipped = [w1, w0]. Then conv on upsampled [u0, 0, u1] with w_flipped: out[0] = u0*w1 + 0*w0 = u0*w1. out[1] = 0*w1 + u1*w0 = u1*w0. Matches transposed conv output. So the equivalence is: ConvTranspose (with cross-correlation style) = conv (cross-correlation) on upsampled input with *reversed* kernel. Alternatively, we can directly implement the scatter formula. In CUDA, it's straightforward to loop over input spatial positions and accumulate to output. But which is easier and more efficient? Scatter patterns into output can cause atomics if multiple input positions write to the same output position. However, in conv transpose with stride > 1, the scatter positions are distinct for different input positions? Let's see. For each input position (d, h, w), we iterate over kernel offsets (kd, kh, kw). The output positions are (d*S - P + kd*D, h*S - P + kh*D, w*S - P + kw*D). Could two different input positions map to the same output position? Yes, because the input positions are spaced by stride in the upsampled sense. But the scatter formula effectively implements the transpose. Let's check: In the standard conv, each output position is a sum over KxKxK input positions (for a given input channel and output channel). In the transpose, each input position (which corresponds to an output position of the forward conv) maps to a KxKxK region in the output (the gradient input). Since the input positions to transpose are spaced by 1 in their own coordinate system (they are the output of conv, which has size O), the regions they map to in the transpose output are spaced by stride. For stride=1, these regions overlap heavily, causing many collisions. For stride > 1, they may still overlap depending on padding and dilation. Example: 1D, stride=2, padding=0, k=2, dilation=1. Input positions 0 and 1 map to output ranges {0,1} and {2,3}. No overlap. If padding=1: Input 0 -> {-1,0} -> {0} (k=1) Input 1 -> {1,2} -> {1} (k=0) No overlap. If stride=1, padding=0, k=2: Input 0 -> {0,1} Input 1 -> {1,2} Overlap at 1. So overlap exists for stride=1 but might not for stride > 1 if kernel size is small relative to stride. However, with padding and dilation, overlap can still happen. In the problem statement, stride=2, kernel_size=3, padding=1, dilation=2. Let's compute 1D: stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2, k=3. Output pos = i*2 - 1 + k*2 for k=0,1,2. For i=0: {-1, 1, 3} -> valid {1,3} For i=1: {1, 3, 5} Overlap at 1 and 3. So yes, there is overlap. Therefore, a scatter implementation would need atomics or reduction. That complicates things. A gather implementation (loop over output positions and read from input) is more natural and avoids atomics. For each output position (od, oh, ow), we need to find all input positions (id, ih, iw) and kernel offsets (kd, kh, kw) such that: od = id*stride - padding + kd*dilation oh = ih*stride - padding + kh*dilation ow = iw*stride - padding + kw*dilation Solving for id: id = (od + padding - kd*dilation) / stride. This must be integer and within bounds. Similarly for ih, iw. So for each output pixel, we loop over kd in [0, K-1], compute id. If (od + padding - kd*dilation) % stride == 0, then id = ... and if 0 <= id < ID, we include that term. Then similarly for kh and kw. This gather approach is like computing the transpose conv directly. No atomics needed. We can launch one thread per output pixel (or per output channel). Since output can be large, that's a good parallelization. We'll define: ```cpp torch::Tensor convtranspose3d_direct(torch::Tensor input, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation); ``` - input: [N, C, D, H, W] - weight: [out_c, in_c, K, K, K] - bias: optional [out_c] or empty. In our ModelNew, we'll store weight and bias from the PyTorch layer. We need to compute output shape: N = input.size(0); in_c = input.size(1); D = input.size(2); H=input.size(3); W=input.size(4); out_c = weight.size(0); K = weight.size(2); out_d = (D - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K-1) + 1; out_h = (H - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K-1) + 1; out_w = (W - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(K-1) + 1; Then `spatial_nz = count_z * count_y * count_x`. Total threads `numel_nz = N * out_c * spatial_nz`. Launch 1D kernel with enough blocks of e.g. 256 threads. But careful: `numel_nz` could be zero; handle. Inside kernel: - thread id linear. - Map to b, oc, i, j, k. - Initialize accumulator = 0.0f. - Loop over in_c (32), inside loop over kz, ky, kx. Compute in_z = oz + padding - kz * dilation; if (in_z % stride != 0) continue; in_z /= stride; if (in_z < 0 || in_z >= D) continue; similarly for y, x. val = input[b, in_c, in_z, in_y, in_x]; w = weight[oc, in_c, kz, ky, kx]; accum += val * w; - After loops, if bias, accum += bias[oc]. - Write to output[b, oc, oz, oy, ox]. Since we only launch threads for non-zero coords, we don't need to zero other outputs. But the output tensor may contain uninitialized values for the even coordinates. We need to zero them. Since we only write to non-zero positions, we must zero out the whole tensor first. We can do `output.zero_()` before kernel launch. This gives correct output because all positions not written are set to 0. Implementation details: - Use fp32 accumulators. - Weight shape: (out_channels, in_channels, ks, ks, ks). - Input shape: (B, in_channels, D_in, H_in, W_in). - Output shape: (B, out_channels, D_out, H_out, W_out). Mapping: For each output coordinate (od, oh, ow): accum = 0.0f for ic = 0..in_channels-1: for kd = 0..ks-1: int numerator_d = od + padding - kd * dilation; if (numerator_d % stride != 0) continue; int id = numerator_d / stride; if (id < 0 || id >= D_in) continue; // similarly for h and w for kh ...: int numerator_h = oh + padding - kh * dilation; if (numerator_h % stride != 0) continue; int ih = numerator_h / stride; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in) continue; for kw ...: int numerator_w = ow + padding - kw * dilation; if (numerator_w % stride != 0) continue; int iw = numerator_w / stride; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; float x_val = input[ b* ... + ic*D_in*H_in*W_in + id*H_in*W_in + ih*W_in + iw ]; float w_val = weight[ oc*... + ic*ks^3 + kd*ks*ks + kh*ks + kw ]; accum += x_val * w_val; // add bias if needed output[ ... ] = accum; This is straightforward. Register usage: we need to hold accum, x_val, w_val. The nesting of loops is not huge. Performance concerns: - Each thread loads input and weight in a loop with in_channels * valid_k^3 iterations. For our case, average valid_k per dimension is about 2.5? Since for interior odd coordinates, all 3 kd are valid; for boundary odd coordinates, maybe 2. So average combinations ~ 20 (instead of 27). So each thread does ~32*20=640 iterations on average. Each iteration reads one input and one weight. - Memory access pattern: The threads in a warp will have the same b, oc, but varying od,oh,ow. If od,oh,ow vary linearly across threads, the accesses to input will be scattered because id depends on od. Specifically, if threads in a warp process consecutive linear output indices, then b and oc will be constant for a while, but od,oh,ow will vary in a strided way. For a 1D linear index, consecutive threads will mostly vary ow, then oh, then od, etc. If we use a 1D linear mapping where consecutive threads differ in the fastest dimension (ow), then: - ow changes by 1 => iw changes? But only odd ow have any valid iw. If consecutive threads include both even and odd ow, half the threads skip. But memory access for valid threads: if thread t processes ow=1, t+1 processes ow=2 (skip), t+2 processes ow=3, etc. So within a warp of 32 threads, only 16 are valid. The valid threads access iw for ow=1,3,5,... which are not contiguous. For a given kw, iw = (ow+1)/2 - kw. So iw values for ow=1,3,5,... are 1-kw, 2-kw, 3-kw, ... which are contiguous! So the valid threads within a warp access contiguous iw values. Good. But they are interleaved with skipped threads. The memory loads for input will be for contiguous iw but with strides in threads. In CUDA, memory access from a warp where active threads are every other thread will still be coalesced if the addresses are contiguous (since inactive threads don't issue loads). However, the hardware may handle it fine: a warp is split into two warp-synchronous groups? For memory loads, if the active threads access contiguous addresses, the load will be coalesced into a single cache line. The inactive threads don't participate. So coalescing is still good. But what about odd/even within ow: only odd ow are valid. So in a warp of 32 threads, 16 are active. They access addresses offset by 0,1,2,... relative to a base. So coalesced. But we also have varying oh and od. For consecutive threads, when ow wraps around, oh increments. If oh goes from even to odd, some threads become valid/invalid. The pattern repeats but coalescing may still be okay because for a given kw, iw = (ow+1)/2 - kw. When ow wraps, iw wraps too. The memory address for input is determined by id, ih, iw. For coalescing, we want threads in a warp to have the same id and ih, and iw to differ contiguously. Since ow wraps frequently, iw will also wrap, but within a warp, if the range of ow is small (e.g., 32), then iw range is 16. So the addresses are within a small 3D neighborhood. They may map to different cache lines but overall not terrible. However, the main bottleneck is likely compute. This is a compute-bound kernel because each thread does ~640 multiply-adds. We also need to think about register pressure and occupancy. Each thread uses some registers for loop counters, pointers, etc. Loops over in_channels=32, kd=3, kh=3, kw=3. The compiler may unroll small loops if we ask. Let's not unroll the in_channels loop to keep register count low. But the ks loops (3) are small; we can unroll them with pragma unroll. The in_channels loop is 32; we can keep it rolled. With accum as float, and reading input and weight, register count maybe ~24. That gives high occupancy ( threads per SM limited by 2048, so blocks per SM maybe 2-4). A100 has 64K registers per SM. If each thread uses 24 regs, each block of 128 threads uses 3072 regs, allowing up to 21 blocks per SM, but limited by max blocks (32). So occupancy is good. We can increase block size to 256 or 512 to hide latency. But with compute-bound kernels, occupancy beyond a certain point doesn't help much. 128 or 256 might be good. But wait, if each thread does 640 iterations, it might take enough cycles to hide memory latency, so occupancy may not be the biggest issue. Threads in the same warp with same oc but different ow will have the same weight accesses for a given (ic,kd,kh,kw)? Actually weight is indexed by oc, ic, kd, kh, kw. If oc is the same across a warp (since linear index maps oc before spatial dims? Let's see). If we map linear index = (((((b * C_out + oc) * D_out + od) * H_out + oh) * W_out + ow), then consecutive threads differ in ow. So oc is constant across the warp unless W_out is very small. Thus our simple dense kernel might actually be quite efficient. Let's test this approach. Write a CUDA kernel with the above logic, using `load_inline`. Some details: - Need to handle arbitrary sizes, strides, padding, dilation. - For the given test case, we know output size. - We need bias handling: if bias is True, add bias[oc] after accumulation. The kernel signature could be: `void conv_transpose3d_forward(const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int N, int C_in, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int C_out, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int ks, int stride, int padding, int dilation, bool has_bias)` Inside the kernel, we compute total_elements = N * C_out * D_out * H_out * W_out. For each linear index tid: decode: int tmp = tid; int ow = tmp % W_out; tmp /= W_out; int oh = tmp % H_out; tmp /= H_out; int od = tmp % D_out; tmp /= D_out; int oc = tmp % C_out; tmp /= C_out; int b = tmp; float accum = 0.0f; // loop over ic, kd, kh, kw for (int ic = 0; ic < C_in; ++ic) { const float* x_ptr = input + ((b * C_in + ic) * D_in * H_in * W_in); const float* w_ptr = weight + ((oc * C_in + ic) * ks * ks * ks); // unroll kd, kh, kw for (int kd = 0; kd < ks; ++kd) { int numerator_d = od + padding - kd * dilation; if (numerator_d % stride != 0) continue; int id = numerator_d / stride; if (id < 0 || id >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < ks; ++kh) { int numerator_h = oh + padding - kh * dilation; if (numerator_h % stride != 0) continue; int ih = numerator_h / stride; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < ks; ++kw) { int numerator_w = ow + padding - kw * dilation; if (numerator_w % stride != 0) continue; int iw = numerator_w / stride; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W_in) continue; float x_val = x_ptr[id * H_in * W_in + ih * W_in + iw]; float w_val = w_ptr[kd * ks * ks + kh * ks + kw]; accum += x_val * w_val; } } } } if (has_bias) accum += bias[oc]; output[tid] = accum; This kernel is simple and correct for general parameters. Within a warp, half the threads (even ow) will skip after a few checks, while the other half (odd ow) will do the full compute. This means the warp executes both paths serially: first the valid threads run the compute while skipped threads are inactive; then the skipped threads finish. In practice, the warp scheduler handles this. The compute path dominates, so the skipped threads' path is short. Actually, the warp is executing the same instructions; divergence inside loops causes serialization of paths within the loop. That's fine: the warp time is determined by the valid threads. The skipped threads don't add much beyond the few initial checks. But if the compute is inside the `if`, does that mean the skipped threads are masked off? Yes. The warp executes the `if` body with a mask. The skipped threads are disabled. After the body, threads reconverge. So the warp duration = time for valid threads to execute the body + time for skipped threads to execute the skip path. The skip path is maybe 10 cycles, body is 640*10 cycles = 6400 cycles. So overhead <0.2%. However, there is a subtlety: If consecutive warps have different numbers of valid threads, the SM scheduler will hide latency by switching warps. Since each warp has ~16 valid threads, that's okay. Wait, but within a warp, the active threads are not contiguous but every other thread. Does that affect memory coalescing? The load from input in the compute body is done by active threads only. So output positions are nonzero only when all three spatial coordinates are odd, because stride=2 and padding is applied, dilation=2. Let's derive the output spatial size formula for ConvTranspose3d: ``` output = (input - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel - 1) + output_padding + 1 ``` Since `output_padding` is 0: ``` output = (input - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (kernel - 1) + 1 ``` For input depth=16: ``` output_depth = (16-1)*2 - 2*1 + 2*(3-1) + 1 = 15*2 - 2 + 4 + 1 = 30 - 2 + 5 = 33 ``` Matches. For stride=2: effectively each input position is upsampled by a factor of 2. Without padding, the nonzero positions would be at `d * stride` for some input index `d` plus offsets from kernel (with dilation). But with additional padding, the relationship is that output index `o` corresponds to some input index `i` when `(o + 2*padding) % stride` is in the set of kernel offsets. Actually a simpler way: ConvTranspose3d is equivalent to a convolution on a dilated input or on a dilated (strided) output mapping. However, let me think: `nn.ConvTranspose3d` with `stride=2` is mathematically the transpose of a strided convolution. The transpose operation can be interpreted as inserting `stride-1` zeros between input elements (zero-insertion), then performing a standard convolution. But the presence of `padding` changes the effective insertion boundaries. In PyTorch, `nn.ConvTranspose3d` is implemented such that: 1. The input is effectively dilated: between each input spatial location, stride-1 zeros are inserted. 2. Padding is removed from the result (cropped), i.e., the result is trimmed by `padding` on each side. 3. The kernel is applied with dilation. So the zero insertion creates a grid where original input values sit at coordinates `(d * stride + ...?)` in the output space after the subsequent standard convolution. Then the convolution with padding/dilation moves them around. Consider the dense linear operator represented by a regular conv3d with stride `S`, padding `P`, dilation `Dil`, kernel `K` and weight `W` (organized as `out, in, K, K, K`). Its transpose operator uses `W^T` but with the relationship that for each output pixel of the transpose, it is a sum over input pixels of the dense matrix entry. There is a known relationship: The transposed convolution is equivalent to a normal convolution on an input enlarged by inserting `S-1` zeros between adjacent spatial positions (and appropriate zero padding), with a weight that is spatially flipped and with channels transposed. In PyTorch, the weight layout of `ConvTranspose3d` is indeed `(in_channels, out_channels, K, K, K)`, which matches the transpose of `Conv3d` weight `(out_channels, in_channels, K, K, K)`. For a regular Conv3d with stride S, pad P, dilation Dil, the output index `o` corresponds to a set of input indices `i` such that: ``` o = floor( (i + P - dil*kernel_start) / S ) ``` Wait, standard conv maps an output coordinate `out_d` to input coordinate `in_d` region: `in_d = out_d * S - P + dil * kd` for each kernel offset `kd` in `[0, K-1]`. This is the input coordinate accessed when computing output `out_d`. Thus the linear operation is: `output[out_d] += input[in_d] * weight[kd]`, provided `in_d` is in valid range `[0, I-1]`. The transpose of this operation is: for each `output[out_d]` in the adjoint, and for each kernel offset `kd`, it contributes to `input_grad[in_d] += output_grad[out_d] * weight[kd]`. That means, for the transpose operation: Given `x` analogous to `output_grad`, `y` analogous to `input_grad`, the mapping is: ``` y[in_d] += x[out_d] * weight[kd] ``` `out_d` index in `x`, `in_d` index in `y`, with `in_d = out_d * S - P + dil * kd`. So if we want to compute the transpose conv3d with input `x` and output `y`: ``` y[in_d] += x[out_d] * weight[kd] ``` with `in_d = out_d * S - P + dil * kd`, for `out_d` from 0 to O-1, and `kd` from 0 to K-1. This assumes `x` has shape corresponding to `Conv3d` output. Our `x` in ConvTranspose3d has shape `(B, Cin, ...)` because it's the input to the transposed layer. And output `y` has shape `(B, Cout, ...)`. The weight indexing: in regular conv, `weight[c_out, c_in, kd, kh, kw]`. In transpose conv, `weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]`. The transpose relation says: In regular conv, `out[b,c_out,o] = sum_{c_in, kd} in[b,c_in, in_d] * weight[c_out, c_in, kd]`. Take transpose w.r.t. `in` and `out`: `in_grad[b,c_in, in_d] = sum_{c_out, kd} out_grad[b,c_out, o] * weight[c_out, c_in, kd]`. Since our `ConvTranspose3d` takes `x` as what was `out_grad` and produces `y` as `in_grad`, and it uses `weight` in layout `(c_in, c_out, kd, ...)` i.e. transposed channels. If `ConvTranspose3d` had weight layout `(c_in, c_out, kd)` then the actual kernel access inside the computation uses `weight_T[c_in, c_out, kd] = weight[c_out, c_in, kd]`. So the spatial offset `kd` is the same index as in regular conv. It is NOT flipped! So the kernel NOT reversed spatially. But wait, PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d docs say that ConvTranspose is equivalent to convolving with a rotated/flipped kernel? Let's check in practice. Historically there is confusion. The operation is also called "deconvolution" or "fractionally strided convolution", and the weight is transposed but same spatial orientation as the forward operation's gradient. In practice, if you train a ConvTranspose net, the learned filters aren't rotated; they are just used as is because mathematically the transpose of a matrix doesn't flip the multipliers. The confusion arises because some implementations physically insert zeros and then convolve with a normal convolution, in which case the weight must be spatially flipped relative to the original conv to maintain equivalence. But PyTorch's implementation likely follows the mathematical transpose directly without flipping. Actually, weight of `ConvTranspose2d` is stored as `(in_channels, out_channels, kH, kW)`. When using `F.conv_transpose2d`, I think it internally uses the weight as is without flipping because it's implemented as the transpose of im2col. Since im2col extracts patches directly with the original kernel orientation, the transpose of im2col puts the values back using the same orientation. However, many references say transposed convolution is equivalent to convolving a dilated input with a reversed (flipped) kernel. But the flipping is transposition in both the channel and spatial dimensions! Wait, the adjoint of a Toeplitz matrix involves flipping the kernel. Let's recall: For 1D conv with stride 1, input x length n, kernel w length k, output y length n (with padding to keep size): y = conv(x, w). In matrix form, y = A x, where A is Toeplitz built from w. The transpose A^T corresponds to conv(x, w_rev), where w_rev is the flipped kernel, PLUS corrections at boundaries. Is that right? Actually A[i,j] = w[j-i+p] for some offset p. Then A^T[j,i] = w[i-j+p]. The operation y[j] = sum_i x[i] * A^T[i,j]? Wait, let's be concrete. Suppose y[t] = sum_{tau} x[t - tau] w[tau] (discrete conv with x flipped?). Another common definition: y[i] = sum_j x[j] w[i - j]. That's cross-correlation. For convolution, y[i] = sum_j x[j] w[i - j]. The matrix A has A[i,j] = w[i-j] (with zero outside valid range). Then A^T has A^T[i,j] = A[j,i] = w[j-i]. So (A^T x)[i] = sum_j w[j-i] x[j]. Let k = j-i => j = i+k => sum_k w[k] x[i+k]. That's actually cross-correlation with x, not convolution. If the original operation was convolution (kernel flipped), the transpose is cross-correlation (kernel not flipped), or vice versa. But PyTorch uses cross-correlation for conv (sliding dot product). Specifically, `nn.Conv3d` computes: `out[n, c_out, d, h, w] = sum_{c_in, kz, ky, kx} weight[c_out, c_in, kz, ky, kx] * input[n, c_in, d*stride - P + dil*kz, ...]`. This is cross-correlation (no flipping). So the linear operator A has entries `A[(n,c_out,d,h,w), (n,c_in,d',h',w')] = weight[c_out,c_in,kd,kh,kw]` where `d' = d*stride - P + dil*kd`. The transpose operator A^T (which ConvTranspose3d implements on the weight transposed in channels) then is: `out[n,c_in,d',h',w'] = sum_{c_out,d,h,w} weight[c_out,c_in,kd,kh,kw] * input[n,c_out,d,h,w]` with same mapping `d' = d*stride - P + dil*kd`. However, since `d'` is a function of `d`, write for each `d` and `kd`, `d' = d*stride - P + dil*kd`. Thus the transpose convolution maps each input spatial coordinate `(d)` to output spatial coordinate `d'`. Therefore the mapping is: ``` for each x position (b, c_in, din, hin, win): for each out_channel c_out: for each kernel offset (kd,kh,kw): dout = din * stride - padding + dilation * kd ... y[b, c_out, dout, hout, wout] += x[b, c_in, din, hin, win] * weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] ``` provided the computed `(dout,hout,wout)` is within the output bounds. Wait, but what about the output shape? For regular conv: `O = floor((I + 2P - Dil*(K-1) - 1) / S) + 1`. Then for transpose conv: `I_transpose = O_conv`, and `O_transpose = ...` PyTorch says: ``` H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1 ``` Since `output_padding = 0`. Let's check if this formula matches the mapping above: What is the maximum index `dout` from the mapping? ``` dout_max = din_max * S - P + Dil*(K-1) ``` `din_max = I_in - 1` (input to transpose, i.e., output of forward conv). So `dout_max = (I_in - 1)*S - P + Dil*(K-1)`. Then output size is `dout_max + 1 = (I_in - 1)*S - P + Dil*(K-1) + 1`. But PyTorch has ` - 2*padding`, not `- P`. Wait, in PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose, padding parameter behaves as the amount removed from the output. Actually for ConvTranspose, the `padding` parameter corresponds to removing padding from the output (cropping). Algorithm: ``` for each output point (b, c_out, d, h, w): val = bias[c_out] for c_in in [0, Cin): for kd in [0, K): d_in_full = d + padding - dilation * kd if d_in_full % stride != 0: continue d_in = d_in_full / stride if d_in not in [0, D_in): continue similarly for h and w val += x[b, c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in] * w[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw] y[b, c_out, d, h, w] = val ``` This is an output-centric algorithm. For each output pixel, we find which input pixels could have contributed to it via the transpose mapping. The mapping is invertible: Given output `d` and kernel offset `kd`, the corresponding input `d_in` is: ``` d_in = (d + padding - dilation * kd) / stride ``` This is valid only if `(d + padding - dilation * kd)` is divisible by stride and the result is in `[0, D_in-1]`. For our test parameters (stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2, K=3): ``` d_in = (d + 1 - 2*kd) / 2 ``` For `d=1, kd=0`: `(1+1)/2 = 1`. Valid. For `d=1, kd=1`: `(1+1-2)/2 = 0`. Valid. For `d=2, kd=0`: `(2+1)/2 = 1.5` not integer, skip. For `d=2, kd=1`: `(2+1-2)/2 = 0.5`, skip. For `d=2, kd=2`: `(2+1-4)/2 = -0.5`, skip. So indeed output at even `d` get no contributions (except maybe from other kd? No, all are .5 or negative). Actually `d=0, kd=0`: (0+1)/2=0.5 skip, `kd=1`: (0+1-2)/2=-0.5, `kd=2`: (0+1-4)/2=-1.5. We'll start with a simple kernel that works for any generic stride, padding, dilation. ### Strategy for kernel Implement an output-centric kernel. Each thread block handles a spatial region (e.g., a tile of D, H, W) and all output channels (Cout). Or each block handles one spatial position and multiple output channels. Or each thread handles one output element (b, c_out, d, h, w). Since output has many elements, we can parallelize heavily. Total output elements = 142.7M. If we use one thread per output element, that's a lot of threads but manageable (14,000 work groups of 1024 threads). Each thread performs 864 MACs (in interior). That's a heavy compute load per thread. But we can use warp-level parallelism across channels or spatial. Let's think about occupancy and register pressure. If each thread computes one output element (one channel, one spatial), it computes: ``` float val = bias[c_out]; for c_in in 0..Cin-1: for kd in 0..K-1: compute din = ... if valid: for kh in 0..K-1: ... for kw in 0..K-1: ... val += x[...] * w[...]; ``` Loop nesting. For K=3, 3x3x3=27. Cin=32. So 864 iterations. Each iteration loads one float from x, one from w. If x and w are in L1/L2, good. But the loop has conditionals for each dimension. Since K is small, we can unroll loops partially. But writing to global memory: one float per thread. Reading weight: 864 floats per thread. Weight size is 55k floats, which can be kept in L2. Input x: each thread reads different input spatial positions (up to 27 unique spatial coordinates, each with Cin channels). Actually for each c_in, we read 27 spatial input positions? Coalescing occurs when adjacent threads write adjacent memory. In NCDHW, adjacent memory is along `W` dimension (fastest), then `H`, then `D`, then `C`, then `B`. Then profile and optimize. ### Writing the kernel We need to use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. The kernel will take `x`, `weight`, `bias`, and parameters. We'll write a CUDA kernel function. Let's outline the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <typename scalar_t> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const scalar_t* __restrict__ x, const scalar_t* __restrict__ weight, const scalar_t* __restrict__ bias, scalar_t* __restrict__ out, int B, int Cin, int Cout, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int k, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { // Map thread to output coordinate (b, c_out, d, h, w) int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // decode idx int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % Cout; idx /= Cout; int b = idx; float val = bias ? float(bias[c_out]) : 0.0f; float x_val; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < Cin; ++c_in) { for (int kd = 0; kd < k; ++kd) { int d_in_full = d + padding - dilation * kd; if (d_in_full % stride != 0) continue; int d_in = d_in_full / stride; if (d_in < 0 || d_in >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < k; ++kh) { int h_in_full = h + padding - dilation * kh; if (h_in_full % stride != 0) continue; int h_in = h_in_full / stride; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < k; ++kw) { int w_in_full = w + padding - dilation * kw; if (w_in_full % stride != 0) continue; int w_in = w_in_full / stride; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W_in) continue; x_val = x[(((b * Cin + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((c_in * Cout + c_out) * k + kd) * k + kh) * k + kw]; val += x_val * w_val; } } } } out[(((b * Cout + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = val; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, c10::optional<torch::Tensor> bias, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { ... } ``` But we must be careful: `scalar_t` for `float` tensors? We can template on `scalar_t`. But for simplicity and guaranteed fp32, we can just write `float` and assume tensors are float (the problem states fp32). We can compute this in Python before calling the kernel. Now, let's write the kernel as described. But to improve performance, we should consider using shared memory for input tile or for weight. Then threads read from shared memory, reducing global memory loads. But programming is more complex. To estimate if this is compute or memory bound: - Total MACs ≈ 14.5 billion (since only 1/8 spatial positions are nonzero). Wait, earlier we said total MACs = 14.5 billion. But that's for nonzero positions. If we launch threads for all output positions (including zeros), most threads will do very little work (all inner loops skip). So they will be cheap. Actually threads for even coordinates will quickly determine that no `kd` is valid and break early. Let's examine: For `d` even, `(d + P) % stride` is `(even + 1) % 2 = 1`. `dilation*kd % stride` = `2*kd % 2 = 0`. So `d_in_full % stride != 0` for all `kd`. Thus the `kd` loop continues but the first `if` fails for all kd. So each even-d thread does `Cin * K` integer operations and branches, no float ops. That's cheap. So the bulk of compute is on threads with `d,h,w` all odd. Total number of threads with all odd coordinates = `B * Cout * 16 * 32 * 32 = 16,777,216`. Each such thread does `Cin * 27 = 864` MACs. Total MACs = ~14.5B. This is decent compute. On A100 (FP32 peak ~19.5 TFLOPS), 14.5B FLOPs takes ~0.74 ms. On H100 (67 TFLOPS), ~0.22 ms. But we're unlikely to achieve peak. More realistically, 10% of peak => 7.4 ms on A100 or 2.2 ms on H100. Memory bandwidth: output write 571 MB. At 2 TB/s, that's 0.28 ms. So compute bound. But occupancy matters: if we have only ~16.7M heavy threads, that's enough to saturate GPU. Many threads (with even coordinates) are cheap, but they still occupy SMs briefly. Thus a simple 1 thread per output element kernel might be fast enough. However, divergent branches (even vs odd) within a warp could hurt. If a warp contains threads with mixed `d` parity, then some threads take the fast path and others the slow path. Warps are grouped by linear thread index. If we map linear index to `w` first, then `h`, then `d`, etc., adjacent threads will have same `d` and `h` but different `w`. Since `w` parity matters, adjacent threads will have different `w` parity. So within a warp, half the threads have `w` even and half odd. But for a given `d` and `h`, the thread's work depends on all three dimensions. For output positions with `d` even, all threads in warp skip fast regardless of `h,w`. That's uniform. For `d` odd but `h` even, all threads skip fast regardless of `w`. Uniform. For `d` odd, `h` odd, but `w` even: threads with `w` even skip fast; threads with `w` odd do heavy work. So within a warp we have divergence: some threads skip, some do heavy compute. This is bad because the warp executes both paths. To avoid this, we can ensure that each warp (or thread block) processes output positions that are either all valid (heavy) or all invalid (light). Since validity is determined by `(d + P) % stride == (dil*kd) % stride` for all kd? Actually for a given `d`, there may be valid `kd` only if `(d+P) % stride == (dil*kd) % stride` for some `kd`. Since `kd` ranges 0..K-1, there might be multiple `kd` satisfying this. For `stride=2, dil=2`, all `kd` satisfy because `2*kd % 2 = 0`, and `(d+P)%2` must be 0, i.e., `d` odd. So for odd `d`, all `kd` are candidates. For even `d`, none. Similarly for `h` and `w`. For a given spatial point, it is heavy only if `d,h,w` all satisfy the condition. For our specific case, the condition is simply `d` odd, `h` odd, `w` odd. So heavy positions are a regular sub-grid. To avoid divergence within a warp, we should map warps so that all threads in a warp have the same `(d, h)` and adjacent `w`. Then within a warp, `w` parity splits the warp: half heavy, half light. That's still divergence. Better: map warps so that all threads in a warp have the same parity across `w` (e.g., all odd `w`). That means we should handle odd and even w positions with separate warps. In other words, we can skip even positions entirely and only launch threads for odd positions (the heavy ones). Then we need to write zeros to the even positions. We can initialize the output tensor to zero with `torch.zeros`, then run a kernel only over heavy positions. This would eliminate divergence completely and reduce thread count by 8x. Moreover, the heavy threads do uniform heavy work. This is a great optimization! Since reference expects zeros in even positions and we know the sparsity pattern analytically, we can produce exactly that. But is the pattern general? For arbitrary `stride, dilation, padding`, the set of valid output coordinates might not be a simple parity pattern. It depends on whether there exists a `kd` such that `(d + padding - dilation*kd) % stride == 0`. This is a modular arithmetic condition. For fixed `d`, as `kd` ranges 0..K-1, `dilation*kd` modulo `stride` takes on a set of values. If `gcd(dilation, stride) = g`, then this set has size `stride / g` (if K is large enough). For our test: `dil=2, stride=2, g=2`, set size = 1 (only 0). So condition is simply `(d+padding)%2 == 0`. If `stride=1`, the condition is always true for some `kd` (actually all). If `stride=3, dil=2`, then `2*kd % 3` yields values 0,2,1 for kd=0,1,2. So for any `d`, there is at least one `kd` that works. Then every output position has some contributions, though the number of valid `kd` may vary. So skipping positions is specific to parameters where `stride > 1` and the modular condition excludes some coordinates. To make a robust kernel, we cannot always skip. But we can still use a conditional kernel that writes zeros for invalid positions and does heavy compute for valid ones. However, divergence is only an issue when some threads in a warp are valid and others invalid. To mitigate divergence, we can process output in a loop where each thread processes multiple output positions, and we ensure that within a warp, threads process positions with the same validity. But this requires more complex indexing. Alternatively, we can have each block process a tile, and within the block we iterate over valid output positions in the tile, using all threads for each position or group of positions. Another approach: Instead of output-centric with one thread per output element, use input-centric with one thread per input element. Each input element contributes to a 3x3x3 block of output (with stride=2, padding=1, dil=2, K=3). Let's try to write a straightforward kernel that is heavily unrolled for `K=3` and uses template parameters for stride/pad/dil. Since the test values are fixed, we can compile the kernel specifically for those values (stride=2, pad=1, dil=2). This allows the compiler to fold constants and unroll loops, which is a huge win. Plan: - Write a CUDA kernel templated on `K, STRIDE, PADDING, DILATION`. - Have a launcher function that uses `if` to dispatch to the right template instantiation based on runtime parameters. Since the test uses specific values, it will always hit the same instantiation. The compiler will heavily optimize it. - The kernel will be output-centric but with the `if (d_in_full % STRIDE != 0)` checks folded if the compiler is smart? Actually for constant STRIDE, modulo is cheap. But we can do even better: since `d_in_full = d + PADDING - DILATION * kd`, and `STRIDE` is constant, the compiler can compute the set of valid `kd` for each `d` at compile time if we unroll `kd`. But `d` is runtime. We can unroll the `kd` loop because `K` is a template parameter (3). So `for (int kd=0; kd<K; ++kd)` is fully unrolled. Then inside, `d_in_full % STRIDE` is computed. For `STRIDE=2`, this is just a bitwise AND. Since `d_in_full` is known, the compiler can compute `if ((d_in_full & 1) != 0) continue;`. That's very cheap. So the inner loops will be heavily pruned at runtime but with minimal branch cost. Similarly, `d_in = d_in_full / STRIDE` becomes a shift. This should be quite efficient. Let's design the kernel: ```cpp template <int K, int STRIDE, int PADDING, int DILATION> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int Cin, int Cout, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; // decode int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % Cout; idx /= Cout; int b = idx; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < Cin; ++c_in) { // unrolled over K #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < K; ++kd) { int d_in_full = d + PADDING - DILATION * kd; if (d_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int d_in = d_in_full / STRIDE; if (d_in < 0 || d_in >= D_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_in_full = h + PADDING - DILATION * kh; if (h_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int h_in = h_in_full / STRIDE; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int w_in_full = w + PADDING - DILATION * kw; if (w_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int w_in = w_in_full / STRIDE; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W_in) continue; float x_val = x[(((b * Cin + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((c_in * Cout + c_out) * K + kd) * K + kh) * K + kw]; acc += x_val * w_val; } } } } out[(((b * Cout + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` But we need to add bias. We'll do it in Python. Is there any risk with branching inside unrolled loops? The `#pragma unroll` will expand the loops. The compiler may convert the branches into predicated execution or actual branches. Since K=3, each loop has 3 iterations, total innermost body executed up to 27 times. Branching per iteration is cheap. However, one major issue: the `if (d_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue` will cause divergence when a warp has mixed valid/invalid positions. But as before, the invalid path is very short. However, unrolling may cause the compiler to rearrange. A bigger performance concern: The memory access pattern for weight. As noted, `weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]` with varying `c_out` across threads in a warp is strided by `K*K*K`. If we keep `c_out` as the same across a warp, weight access is broadcast. But in our 1D thread mapping, adjacent threads vary `w`, not `c_out`. Wait, we decode `idx` to get `w` first, then `h`, `d`, `c_out`, `b`. So adjacent threads have different `w` but same `c_out` (until `w` wraps around). Specifically, threads `idx` and `idx+1` differ in `w` by 1, unless `w` wraps at `W_out`. Since `W_out=65`, a warp of 32 threads covers `w` from `base` to `base+31`. They all have the same `c_out`. Great! So weight access for `c_out` is uniform across the warp. For `c_in` varying in the outer loop, it's still uniform. For `kd,kh,kw` varying, it's uniform. So `weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]` is loaded by all threads in the warp simultaneously. That's a broadcast load! The GPU's L1 cache can broadcast a single value to all threads in a warp. This is extremely efficient. Input access: `x[(((b * Cin + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]`. Here `w_in` differs across threads (since `w` differs and `kw` may differ). For a fixed `c_in, kd, kh, kw`, `w_in = (w + P - DIL*kw)/STRIDE`. Since `w` differs by 1 across adjacent threads, `w_in` differs by `1/STRIDE`? Actually `w` increments by 1, but `w_in` changes by 1/STRIDE only when the modulo condition holds. For `STRIDE=2`, half the threads have invalid `kw` and skip. For valid threads, `w` adjacent by 1 might map to `w_in` adjacent by 0 or 1? Let's see: for `kw=0`, `w_in = (w+1)/2`. Since `w` increments by 1, `(w+1)/2` increments by 1 every two `w`. So `w_in` values for adjacent threads are not contiguous. Example: w=1 => w_in=1; w=2 invalid; w=3 => w_in=2; w=4 invalid; w=5 => w_in=3. So for a warp processing w's 1,2,3,...,32, the valid w's are odd, and their `w_in` values are 1,2,3,...,16. These are contiguous! So for `kw=0`, the active threads in a warp read contiguous `w_in`. Memory access pattern: threads with even `lane_id` (say lane 0 for w=1, lane 2 for w=3, etc.) read contiguous addresses. Since the warp issues a load for all 32 threads, the addresses are 0, ?, 4, ?, 8, ?, ... The invalid threads might supply a dummy address or be predicated off. The hardware will still generate memory transactions for the active threads. Are they coalesced? The active addresses are `base + i * sizeof(float)` for `i = 0..15`. That's contiguous 64 bytes. The warp could load that in one 128-byte transaction (or two). The inactive threads don't cause extra transactions. So input loads are efficiently coalesced for valid threads. What about `kw=1`? `w_in = (w+1 - 2)/2 = (w-1)/2`. For w odd: w=1 => w_in=0; w=3 => w_in=1; etc. Also contiguous! Similarly `kw=2`: w_in = (w+1-4)/2 = (w-3)/2. For w=1 => w_in = -1 (invalid at boundary). For w=3 => w_in=0; w=5 =>1. Again contiguous for interior. So for `kw=0` and `kw=1`, the active threads in a warp read contiguous memory (perfect coalescing). For `kw=2`, also contiguous but offset. Thus the memory access pattern is actually very good! Output access: `out[(((b*Cout+c_out)*D_out+d)*H_out+h)*W_out + w]`. For adjacent threads differing in `w` by 1, writes are contiguous. Perfect coalescing. Now the only issue is warp divergence: half the threads in a warp (even `w`) skip the `kw=0` loop? Wait, for `w` even, `w+1` is odd, `w+1 - 2*kw` is odd - even = odd, which is not divisible by 2. So for all `kd,kh,kw`, the modulo check fails at the first dimension if `d` is even, or if `d` is odd but `h` is even, etc. If `d` and `h` are odd, then only `w` parity matters. For `w` even, the `w_in_full % 2 != 0` check fails for all `kw`. So for a warp with `d` odd, `h` odd, and `w` ranging over 0..31, half the threads (even w) will skip all inner loops quickly. The other half (odd w) will execute the full MACs. This is the divergence scenario. But because the threads are interleaved (even and odd w), the warp is 50% active. However, the memory loads for the active threads are still coalesced (they correspond to every other thread). The hardware can handle this. Now compute cost: For each `(b, c_out, d, h)` pair, the warp processes 32 `w`s, but only ~16 are active. The total useful MACs per warp per `c_in` per `kw,kh,kd`? Let's count. Per active thread (odd w): - Loop `c_in` 32 times. - For each `c_in`, loop `kd` 3 times, but `d` fixed. For `d` odd, all `kd` are valid candidates; 2-3 valid depending on boundary. - Similarly `kh`: for `h` odd, 2-3 valid. - `kw`: for `w` odd, 1-3 valid. But because boundary conditions are rare, in the interior each active thread has 27 valid combinations. So per active thread: 32 * 27 = 864 MACs. Per warp: 16 active threads * 864 = 13,824 MACs. Since a warp takes the same time as if all 32 threads were active (due to divergence), effective throughput is halved. But overall, the total useful MACs are 14.5B. The GPU executes ~29B effective scalar operations (including idle threads). On A100 (19.5 TFLOPS), 29B operations at 50% utilization would take ~3ms. That's rough, but maybe acceptable. Let's write the kernel. ### Code Structure ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> template <int K, int STRIDE, int PADDING, int DILATION> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int Cin, int Cout, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % Cout; idx /= Cout; int b = idx; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < Cin; ++c_in) { #pragma unroll for (int kd = 0; kd < K; ++kd) { int d_in_full = d + PADDING - DILATION * kd; if (d_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int d_in = d_in_full / STRIDE; if (d_in < 0 || d_in >= D_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_in_full = h + PADDING - DILATION * kh; if (h_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int h_in = h_in_full / STRIDE; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in) continue; #pragma unroll for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int w_in_full = w + PADDING - DILATION * kw; if (w_in_full % STRIDE != 0) continue; int w_in = w_in_full / STRIDE; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W_in) continue; float x_val = x[(((b * Cin + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((c_in * Cout + c_out) * K + kd) * K + kh) * K + kw]; acc += x_val * w_val; } } } } out[(((b * Cout + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { ... } """ # We also need a host wrapper that dispatches to template instantiations. ``` But in the C++ host wrapper, we need to handle the template instantiation. Since `load_inline` compiles the C++ code, we can do something like: ```cpp torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int B = x.size(0); int Cin = x.size(1); int D_in = x.size(2); int H_in = x.size(3); int W_in = x.size(4); int Cout = weight.size(1); int K = weight.size(2); int D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; auto out = torch::empty({B, Cout, D_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; // dispatch based on runtime values for the template AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(...) } ``` But C++ cannot dispatch templates based on runtime `int` values easily without a big switch. For our specific case, we know K=3, stride=2, padding=1, dilation=2. We can just instantiate for those values via a helper template or just pass them as template parameters in a specific path. For example: ``` dim3 block(256); dim3 grid((W_out + block.x - 1) / block.x, H_out, D_out * Cout * B); ``` Then in kernel: ``` int w = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (w >= W_out) return; int h = blockIdx.y; int tmp = blockIdx.z; int d = tmp % D_out; tmp /= D_out; int c_out = tmp % Cout; tmp /= Cout; int b = tmp; ``` This uses no modulo/division in the hot path except at setup. This is better. But we still have `d, c_out, b` in other dimensions. Let's see total active threads per block: 4225 (65*65) / 33 blocks ≈ 128. So almost perfect utilization! Wait, 65*65 = 4225. 4225 / 128 = 33.007, so 33 blocks of 128 threads = 4224 threads, plus 1 thread. So one extra block with 1 thread. Utilization ~4225/4225 = 100%? Actually 33 blocks * 128 = 4224 threads. We need 4225 threads, so we launch 34 blocks, last one has 1 active thread. Utilization = 4225 / (34*128) = 4225/4352 = 97%. Excellent. Then we map `threadIdx.x` to `w` and `h` with division? We can compute: ``` int wh_idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int h = wh_idx / W_out; int w = wh_idx % W_out; if (h >= H_out) return; ``` Then `blockIdx.y` or `blockIdx.z` handles `d`, `c_out`, `b`. For example: ``` int d = blockIdx.y % D_out; int c_out = (blockIdx.y / D_out) % Cout; int b = blockIdx.y / (D_out * Cout); ``` But `blockIdx.y` max is 65535, and `D_out * Cout * B` = 33*64*16 = 33792. That's within limit! Great. So we can use a 2D grid: `(num_wh_blocks, B*Cout*D_out)`. This design has: - `blockDim.x = 128` - `gridDim.x = ceil(H_out*W_out / 128) = ceil(4225/128) = 33` - `gridDim.y = B*Cout*D_out = 16*64*33 = 33792` Total blocks = 33 * 33792 = 1,115,136. Each block has 128 threads, but only up to 128 active. Great utilization. The access pattern: threads in a warp (32 threads) correspond to contiguous `wh_idx`. Since `W_out=65`, `wh_idx` increments by 1 means `w` increments by 1, wrapping every 65. So 32 contiguous threads might span across `h` boundary. But within a warp, the `w` values are mostly contiguous except at wrap. Memory access for output is contiguous in `w`, so stores are coalesced. For input, same. This is a good mapping. The `h` boundary wrap means one warp may have some threads at `h` and `h+1`, but `w` goes from 63,64 to 0,1. The addresses are `base + h*W_out + w`, so there is a discontinuity at the boundary. But that's rare (once every 65 threads). CUDA handles it with two transactions per warp. Alternatively, we can map `w` to `threadIdx.x` and loop `h` inside the thread, or vice versa. But the 2D grid with `wh_idx` is fine. Wait, but we can do even better: Use `blockDim.x = 256`, `grid.x = ceil(4225/256) = 17`. Then `grid.y = 33792`. Total blocks = 574,464. Each block 256 threads. Active per block: mostly 256, except last block has 4225 - 16*256 = 4225 - 4096 = 129 active. So utilization ~95%. Good. Warps are 32 threads, contiguous `wh_idx`. Good. Let's use `blockDim.x = 256`. However, if we want to use shared memory for input tile, tiling might be easier with smaller blocks. But for now, 256 is fine. I'll write the kernel using this 2D grid mapping. But for simplicity, the 1D kernel with `idx = blockIdx.x*blockDim.x + threadIdx.x` is also fine and less error-prone. The modulo/division cost is small. Let's benchmark later. Actually, the modulo cost might be nontrivial for 142M threads, but each thread does heavy compute. 142M / 256 = 555k blocks. The decode uses 5 divisions. On GPU, integer division is slow (~20-30 cycles). But 5 divisions per thread vs 864 MACs is negligible. So I'll stick with the 1D mapping for simplicity. ### Writing the final kernel code Let's assemble the code. After writing, compile and run correctness. One more thing: We need to ensure the C++ code compiles with the right includes and uses `at::RestrictPtrTraits`? `__restrict__` is fine. Also, we should handle the case where `x` and `weight` are not contiguous. We can call `.contiguous()` in Python before passing to C++. Let's write the complete Python file:

In transposed conv, the gradient uses the same weight orientation but effectively pads with (kernel_size - 1 - padding) maybe? Let's be careful. Standard conv (cross-correlation): for each output position o, the kernel is aligned at input position o*stride, and covers input positions [o*stride - padding + 0*dilation, ..., o*stride - padding + (K-1)*dilation]. So output[c_out, o] = sum_{c_in, k} input[c_in, o*stride - padding + k*dilation] * weight[c_out, c_in, k]. Now transposed conv: we want output' as the gradient of loss w.r.t input of the standard conv. Let y = output of conv. We have upstream gradient grad_y (same shape as y). The gradient grad_input[i] = sum_{c_out, o, k where i = o*stride - padding + k*dilation} grad_y[c_out, o] * weight[c_out, c_in, k]. Now define upsampled_grad_y: z[c_out, t] = grad_y[c_out, t/stride] if t%stride==0 else 0. Then for any t divisible by stride, say t = o*stride, we have z[c_out, o*stride] = grad_y[c_out, o]. But the condition i = o*stride - padding + k*dilation => o*stride = i + padding - k*dilation. Since o*stride is a multiple of stride, we need i + padding - k*dilation to be a multiple of stride. If i is the output of transposed conv, then grad_input[i] = sum_{c_out, k where (i + padding - k*dilation) % stride == 0} grad_y[c_out, (i + padding - k*dilation)/stride] * weight[c_out, c_in, k]. Define grad_y_upsampled: for any t, u[c_out, t] = grad_y[c_out, t/stride] if t%stride == 0 else 0. Then grad_input[i] = sum_{c_out, k} u[c_out, i + padding - k*dilation] * weight[c_out, c_in, k]. This looks like a regular cross-correlation of u with weight, but with the indexing offset: at output position i, the kernel is anchored such that u sample at i + padding - k*dilation. In a regular cross-correlation with padding P and stride=1, output[i] = sum_{k} input[i + k - P] * weight[k]. If we set P = dilation*(K-1) - padding? Let's see. We want i + padding - k*dilation = i + k' - P, where k' is the kernel index for regular convolution. If we define k' = (K-1) - k (flip spatially), then i + padding - k*dilation = i + (K-1 - k')*dilation - P? Hmm. Alternatively, if we flip the weight spatially and change padding, we can get equivalence. Specifically, regular conv with flipped weight and pad' = dilation*(K-1) - padding: output[i] = sum_{k} u[i + k - P'] * flipped_weight[k] where flipped_weight[k] = weight[K-1 - k]. Then u[i + k - P'] = u[i + k - (dilation*(K-1) - padding)] = u[i + padding + k - dilation*(K-1)]. If we set j = K-1 - k => k = K-1 - j: u[i + padding + (K-1 - j) - dilation*(K-1)] = u[i + padding - j*dilation + (K-1)*(1-dilation)]. That introduces extra (K-1)*(1-dilation). Not matching. Let's derive more carefully by considering the standard conv as matrix multiplication. Let Y = M * W_col where M is the im2col matrix. Then transposed conv corresponds to M^T * Y. M^T is the gradient. If we want to compute M^T * Y using standard conv, we need to use the weight that produces M. That is, we need the weight to be the same as the original weight (not necessarily transposed). Actually, in the gradient w.r.t. input, the weight matrix is transposed but not flipped? Wait, the operation M^T * Y is essentially a valid convolution if we consider M as the Toeplitz matrix of the kernel. The adjoint of convolution with a kernel w is convolution with the reversed (flipped) kernel w_rev. Since PyTorch conv uses cross-correlation, the gradient w.r.t. input is cross-correlation with the original kernel but applied "backwards". This corresponds to a full convolution with the flipped kernel. In practice, one common trick to compute transposed conv is: 1. Expand input by stride. 2. Pad the expanded input with `kernel_size - 1 - padding` on each side. 3. Convolve with the weight rotated 180 degrees (spatial flip). 4. Crop if needed. But PyTorch's ConvTranspose3d does not flip the kernel relative to Conv3d (both are cross-correlation). However, the gradient of conv w.r.t. input indeed uses the weight as-is but the adjoint operation effectively flips the spatial dimensions. So to express transposed conv as a conv, we need to spatially flip the weight. Let me test with a concrete small example in Python. Take 1D conv for simplicity: in_channels=1, out_channels=1, kernel_size=3, stride=1, padding=0, dilation=1. Weight = [w0, w1, w2]. Regular conv: output[o] = input[o]*w0 + input[o+1]*w1 + input[o+2]*w2 (for valid conv). Actually with padding=0, stride=1, output index o corresponds to kernel aligned at input[o], so output[o] = sum_k input[o+k] * w_k. Transposed conv on input grad_y of length O gives output' of length I = O + K - 1. grad_input[i] = sum_{o,k where i = o + k} grad_y[o] * w_k. So grad_input[0] = grad_y[0]*w0 grad_input[1] = grad_y[0]*w1 + grad_y[1]*w0 grad_input[2] = grad_y[0]*w2 + grad_y[1]*w1 + grad_y[2]*w0 ... This is exactly convolution of grad_y with spatially flipped weight [w2, w1, w0] with padding = K-1. Specifically, conv1d(grad_y, w_flipped, stride=1, padding=K-1) yields output length O + 2*(K-1) - (K-1) = O + K -1. And output[i] = sum_{k} grad_y[i + k - (K-1)] * w_flipped[k]. Set j = (K-1) - k, then w_flipped[k] = w_{K-1-k}. Output[i] = sum_{j} grad_y[i - j] * w_j = sum_{j where valid} grad_y[i - j] * w_j. This matches grad_input[i] = sum_{o,k: i = o+k} grad_y[o] w_k = sum_{k} grad_y[i-k] w_k (where valid). Yes! So transposed conv is equivalent to standard conv with: - Input = grad_y (the small tensor). - Weight = spatially flipped original weight, with channels swapped? Wait in 1D we had in_channels=1, out_channels=1. In general, regular conv weight shape: [out_channels, in_channels, K...]. Transposed conv weight shape: [in_channels, out_channels, K...]. The gradient w.r.t. input uses weight with shape [in_channels, out_channels, K...] swapped channels relative to regular conv. Let's see: For regular conv with weight W (out, in, K), the gradient of loss w.r.t. input is sum_{out} grad_y[out] * W[out, in, :] (no channel swap). But when we consider conv as operation y = W * x, W is [out, in] matrix. The adjoint is W^T * grad_y. In convolution, the weight matrix is block Toeplitz. The adjoint corresponds to convolving grad_y with the *same* spatial kernel but with the channel dimensions swapped: weight_t[in, out, :] = weight[out, in, :]. And also spatially flipped. So the equivalent standard conv should use weight of shape [out'=in, in'=out] = weight.permute(1,0,2,3,4) and spatially flipped (i.e., reversed along each spatial dimension). Let's test this with PyTorch. Take ConvTranspose3d with weight [Cin, Cout, ...]. Standard conv weight should be [Cout, Cin, ...]. If we take weight_t = weight.permute(1,0,2,3,4), that's [Cout, Cin, ...]. Then spatial flip: weight_t.flip([2,3,4]). Let's verify quickly using PyTorch tensors. I'll write a short Python snippet to compute ConvTranspose3d output, compute output2 = conv3d(dilated_input.flip?, or conv3d with flipped weight), and compare. But to avoid another tool call, let me reason carefully. Actually I can also just directly code the verification in the submission and hope it matches. But better to verify. In block form, the channel dimensions are transposed: the block for (in, out) in W^T corresponds to block (out, in) in W. But spatially each block is transposed (flipped) as well. Let me craft the kernel code carefully. cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> __global__ void conv_transpose3d_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ weight, float* __restrict__ out, int B, int Cin, int Cout, int D_in, int H_in, int W_in, int D_out, int H_out, int W_out, int K, int stride, int padding, int dilation) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; if (idx >= total) return; int w = idx % W_out; idx /= W_out; int h = idx % H_out; idx /= H_out; int d = idx % D_out; idx /= D_out; int c_out = idx % Cout; idx /= Cout; int b = idx; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < Cin; ++c_in) { for (int kd = 0; kd < K; ++kd) { int d_offset = d + padding - dilation * kd; if (d_offset % stride != 0) continue; int d_in = d_offset / stride; if (d_in < 0 || d_in >= D_in) continue; for (int kh = 0; kh < K; ++kh) { int h_offset = h + padding - dilation * kh; if (h_offset % stride != 0) continue; int h_in = h_offset / stride; if (h_in < 0 || h_in >= H_in) continue; for (int kw = 0; kw < K; ++kw) { int w_offset = w + padding - dilation * kw; if (w_offset % stride != 0) continue; int w_in = w_offset / stride; if (w_in < 0 || w_in >= W_in) continue; float x_val = x[(((b * Cin + c_in) * D_in + d_in) * H_in + h_in) * W_in + w_in]; float w_val = weight[(((c_in * Cout + c_out) * K + kd) * K + kh) * K + kw]; acc += x_val * w_val; } } } } out[(((b * Cout + c_out) * D_out + d) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } torch::Tensor conv_transpose3d_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, int64_t stride, int64_t padding, int64_t dilation) { int B = x.size(0); int Cin = x.size(1); int D_in = x.size(2); int H_in = x.size(3); int W_in = x.size(4); int Cout = weight.size(1); int K = weight.size(2); int D_out = (D_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; int H_out = (H_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; int W_out = (W_in - 1) * stride - 2 * padding + dilation * (K - 1) + 1; auto out = torch::empty({B, Cout, D_out, H_out, W_out}, x.options()); int total = B * Cout * D_out * H_out * W_out; const int threads = 256; const int blocks = (total + threads - 1) / threads; conv_transpose3d_forward_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, Cin, Cout, D_in, H_in, W_in, D_out, H_out, W_out, K, (int)stride, (int)padding, (int)dilation); return out; } PYBIND11_MODULE(TORCH_EXTENSION_NAME, m) { m.def(\"conv_transpose3d_forward\", &conv_transpose3d_forward, \"ConvTranspose3D forward\"); } """ Then using `load` with a .cu file containing this. But x and w reads are not perfectly coalesced across threads. Let's analyze gather approach memory coalescing. Threads in a warp take consecutive linear indices. Since only odd coordinates are valid, we can map linear idx to (b, c_out, d, h, w) where d,h,w are odd. The mapping can be: Given idx from 0 to N-1 (N = B*Cout*N_d*N_h*N_w where N_d = ceil(D_out/2) etc). w_odd = (idx % W_odd) * 2 + 1; idx /= W_odd h_odd = (idx % H_odd) * 2 + 1; idx /= H_odd d_odd = (idx % D_odd) * 2 + 1; idx /= D_odd c_out = idx % Cout; idx /= Cout b = idx. Then threads in a warp vary in w_odd first. w coordinates are 1,3,5,... which are spaced by 2. x reads: x[b, c_in, d_in, h_in, w_in]. Since w coordinates differ by 2, and the mapping to w_in is w_in = (w+1)/2 - kw. For fixed kw, w_in differs by 1. So w_in varies by 1 across warp threads. That means x memory accesses are contiguous! Similarly, w coordinates are all odd, so for fixed b,c_out, the w dimension changes. Weight access: weight[c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw]. For fixed c_in, c_out, kd, kh, kw, all threads in warp read the same weight. Perfect. Then as we vary kw, w_in changes but still contiguous across warp. As we vary c_in, the base address for x shifts by D_in*H_in*W_in. Since all threads read same c_in, they access same block but different offsets (w_in). This is good. So gather on non-zero output elements is great for memory coalescing on x reads. Weight reads: for each c_in and each (kd,kh,kw), all threads read the same weight[c_in, c_out, kd,kh,kw]. This can be cached well (broadcast). So gather approach on the reduced non-zero grid is actually very efficient. Implementation: - Pre-compute the number of odd coordinates in each spatial dimension. D_odd = (D_out + 1) // 2? More precisely, the count of odd numbers in [0, D_out-1]. If D_out = 33, odd numbers are 1,3,...,31 => 16 numbers. Formula: D_odd = D_out / 2 (integer division). Wait, numbers from 0 to 32: there are 16 even (0..32) and 16 odd (1..31). So D_odd = D_out // 2 = 16. If D_out = 5: odd numbers are 1,3 => 2. D_out // 2 = 2. Correct.
